CHAPTER X

EKODDIá¹¢á¹¬A AND OTHER ÅšRÄ€DDHAS

Having dealt at great length with PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha which is the model of all Å›rÄddhas it is now time to speak of Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a Å›rÄddha that is a modification of PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha1. The word Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a means ’that in which only one deceased person2 is intended (to be invoked or benefited)’. The PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha is intended for three paternal ancestors and is thus distinguished from Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a. In Å›Än. gr. IV. 2, Baud. gr. III, 12.6, the ÅšrÄddhasÅ«tra of KatyÄyana (kaá¹‡á¸ikÄ 4), and YÄj. I. 251-252, the points of difference between the two are clearly set out3. In this Å›rÄddha only one arghya is offered, there is only one pavitra and only one piá¹‡á¸a is offered, there is no ÄvÄhana (as there is in PÄrvaá¹‡a), no agnaukaraá¹‡a, there are no brÄhmaá¹‡as invited to represent the ViÅ›ve-devas; the question about being satiated by the dinner is to be asked in the word ‘svaditam’ (did it taste well) and the brÄhmaá¹‡as reply with ‘susvaditam’) (it had excellent taste ); instead of saying ‘may it be in exhaustible,’ the formula here used is â€˜May it approach’ (or wait upon the deceased person); when the brÄhmaá¹‡as are sent away at the end of the dinner the word used is ‘be satisfied (or delighted)’ and they reply ‘we are delighted.’ The Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a (1II. 13. 23-26) and MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya (28. 8-11) closely follow the ÅšrÄddhasÅ«tra and YÄj. According to Å›Än. gr. (IV. 2. 7)4, Manu III, 257, MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya (28. 11), YÄj. I. 256 and others, for one year after the death of a twice-born person till the sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a Å›rÄddha is performed this is the way in which Å›rÄddha is to be performed for the preta every month.

Characteristics of Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a

The Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra 21.2 provides that suitable changes (Å«ha) should be made in the mantras employed (e.g. instead of ‘atra pitaro mÄdayadhvam’ the mantra should be â€˜atra pitarmÄdayasva’ (O father!5 regale thyself here). In Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a such a formula as ‘ye ca tvÄm-anu’ (those that come after thee) cannot be employed and the word ‘pitá¹›’ is not to be employed (till sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a is performed), but the word ‘preta’ (AparÄrka p. 525 quoting Åšaunaka-gá¹›hyapariÅ›iá¹£á¹­a)6.

As seen above (p. 262, note 591a) Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a Å›rÄddhas are of three kinds, nava, navamiÅ›ra and purÄá¹‡a. NavaÅ›rÄddhas are those that are performed till the 10th or 11th day after death, navamiÅ›ras (or miÅ›ras) are those that are performed after the 11th day till one year (or six months, according to some) after death. AparÄrka quotes a verse from VyÄghra7 that Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a-Å›rÄddha is performed on the 11th day, on the 4th day, at the end of each month for a year after death and every year on the day of death and a verse of KatyÄyana that (ekoddiá¹£á¹­a) Å›rÄddha should be performed for one who had kept sacred fires on the 11th day after the day of cremation and that the Dhruva Å›rÄddhas should always be performed on the day of death. AparÄrka explains ‘DhruvÄá¹‡i’ as meaning those that are performed after three fortnights from death. About the navaÅ›rÄddha there is again a divergence of views. One meaning has already been given above on p. 262 note 593. The SkandapurÄá¹‡a VI (NÄgarakhaá¹‡á¸a ) 205. 1-4 and GarudapurÄá¹‡a (pretakhaá¹‡á¸a 5. 67-69 ) state that navaÅ›rÄddhas are nine viz. the three performed where a man died, where the funeral procession rested on the way and where the burnt bones were collected and six more performed on the 5th, 7th, 8th, 9th, 10th, and 11th days after death.

Numerous works lay down that there are sixteen Å›rÄddhas which must be performed for a deceased person and that if these are not performed then his spirit is not freed from the condition of being a preta and a piÅ›aca8. There is a great deal of divergence of views about what these sixteen Å›rÄddhas are, some works including sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a among the 16, others excluding it therefrom. Gobhilasmá¹›ti III. 67 enumerates9 the 16 as follows: The twelve monthly Å›rÄddhas (performed every month on the tithi of death), the first (i.e. the sraddha on the 11th day), two Å›rÄddhas on (a day prior to the expiry of) every six months from the tithi of death and sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡Ã  are the sixteen Åšraddhas. The GarudapurÄá¹‡a10 refers to three groups of sixteen, one of which enumerates the 16 sraddhas as those performed on the 12th day (after death), after three fortnights, after six months, after every month and at the end of the year, while the PadmapurÄá¹‡a11 enumerates them as follows: the sixteen Å›rÄddhas are those performed on the 4th day after death, at the end of three fortnights, after six months, after a year and twelve Å›rÄddhas performed each month (on the tithi of death). The Kalpataru p. 25 and BrahmapurÄá¹‡a q. by AparÄrka (p. 523) state that the 16 Å›rÄddhas are those performed on 4th, 5th, 9th and 11th days after death and the twelve monthly Å›rÄddhas (on the tithi of death12).

Views on sixteen Å›rÄddhas

LaugÄká¹£i and others say that after performing the 16 Å›rÄddhas according to the procedure for Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a Å›rÄddhas sapiá¹‡á¸ana should be performed13. The MadanapÄrijÄta (p. 615 ), the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (III. p. 599) and others say that in this conflict of views one should follow the usage of one’s country, Vedic Å›ÄkhÄ or family. There were two views about the Å›rÄddha on the 11th day. It should be remembered that YÄj. III. 22 laid down impurity on death for 10, 12, 15 and 30 days respectively for the four vará¹‡as. One view espoused by Å›ankha and Paithinasi was that in spite of the days of impurity not being over Å›rÄddha must be performed on the 11th day (and for the moment the performer becomes pure for that purpose). The other view was that of the MatsyapurÄá¹‡a and Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra 21.1 that the first Å›rÄddha (ekoddiá¹£á¹­a ) was to be performed on the expiry of ÄÅ›auca.

In the case of ascetics dying it is provided by UÅ›anas14 that by the fact of their taking to the order of sannyÄsa (of the ekadaá¹‡á¸i kind in the Kaliyuga ) they have not to undergo the condition of being preta, no Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a nor Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a should be performed for them by their son or other relative but only pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha on the 11th day and every year thereafter. ÅšatÄtapa provides15 that no Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a, no water, no piá¹‡á¸a and no cremation rites should be offered to an ascetic nor mourning should be observed, but pÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha should be performed. Pracetas quoted by Mit on YÄj. I. 256 states that no ekoddiá¹£á¹­a is to be performed for an ascetic nor sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a, but only pÄrvaá¹‡a on the day of death every year in Bhadrapada dark half. The SivapurÄá¹‡a (KailÄsa-samhitÄ) chapters 22 and 23 deal with the rites to be performed on an ascetic’s death on the 11th and 12th days.

In the NavaÅ›rÄddhas no incense, no lamps are employed. All mantras having the words ‘pitá¹›’ and ‘svadhÄ namaá¸¥’ are omitted and so is the word ‘anu’ omitted and there is no japa and no recital of mantras in the hearing of the brÄhmaá¹‡as. As stated in the BrahmapurÄá¹‡a, the Å›rÄddhas performed in the house at the end of impurity on the 12th day, at the end of a month etc, are called ekoddiá¹£á¹­a. It follows that the rites called navaÅ›rÄddhas offered during the days of impurity are performed at the place of death or in the cemetery or at a place where water and piá¹‡da are offered (and not in the house ); vide Sm. C. (ÄÅ›auca p. 176 ). In the navamiÅ›ra Å›rÄddha mantras are employed according to some. In former times and now also all the 16 Å›rÄddhas are performed on the 11th day. Hardly any one waits for one year to perform Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a. In former times it was laid down (e.g. by the Mit. ) that in times of difficulty, Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a should be performed even before a year elapses after performing the sixteen Å›rÄddhas. But now this exception has become the rule.

Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a or Sapiá¹‡á¸ana is the reception of a deceased person into the community of pitá¹›s to whom piá¹‡á¸as are offered. Several times were prescribed by ancient works for this. Acc. to the Kauá¹£Ä«taki gr. IV. 2 this Å›rÄddha could be performed after the death of the deceased at the end of a year or at the end of three fortnights or on the happening of a lucky event (like the birth of a son or a marriage). The BhÄradvÄja16 gr. (III. 17) allowed it at the end of a year (after death) or in the 11th or 6th or 4th month or on the 12th day. The Baud. Pitá¹›medhasÅ«tra II. 12.1 mentions five times for sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a viz, one year, 11th, 6th or 4th month or 12th day. The GarudapurÄá¹‡a (Pretakhaá¹‡á¸a 6. 53-54) states the time of Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a to be one year, six months, three fortnights, the 12th day or on a lucky occasion. The Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a (III. 13. 26ff) makes similar provisions about sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a (which is an ekoddiá¹£á¹­a Å›rÄddha). AparÄrka (p. 540) after a long discussion holds that there are three times for a performer who is an ÄhitÄgni viz. 12th day, some day between the end of ÄÅ›auca and the first amÄvÄsya (after death) or the first amÄvÄsyÄ after ÄÅ›auca ends and prescribes four times for one who has not kept sacred fires, viz. a year, six months, three fortnights or when a lucky event occurs.

The time for Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a

The MadanapÄrijÄta quotes a verse of VyÄsa that the 12th day is commended (for the sapiá¹‡á¸ana Å›rÄddha) because family usages are innumerable, because man’s life is short and because the body is evanescent. The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (21.20) provides that for Å›Å«dras 12th day after death is the only day for Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a (but without Vedic mantras). Gobhila laid down that after Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a was performed, there was to be no performance of Å›rÄddhas every month, but Gautama (or Åšaunaka, according to AparÄrka p. 543) held the view that they may be performed according to the procedure of ekoddiá¹£á¹­a Å›rÄddhas. Bhattoji says 17 that when sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a is performed before the end of the year, then all the sixteen Å›rÄddhas must be performed before Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a, yet the monthly Å›rÄddhas should again be performed at their proper times for one year. YÄj. I. 255 and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. UÅ›anas provides that even if the heirs of a deceased person are divided in wealth, still the navaÅ›rÄddhas, the sixteen Å›rÄddhas, and sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a must be performed by one alone18 (e.g. the eldest son), but Pracetas provides that when a year has ( elapsed ) Å›rÄddha may be performed separately by each heir19.

The procedure of Sapiá¹‡á¸ana or Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a is described in numerous works such as the SÄn. gr. V. 9, Kauá¹£itaki gr. IV. 2, Baud. Pitá¹›medhasÅ«tra III, 12.12, ÅšrÄddha-sÅ«tra of KÄtyÄyana (kaá¹‡á¸ikÄ 5), YÄj. I. 253-254, Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a III. 13.27 ff., Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. It is briefly set out here20.

As said by Baud.Pitá¹›medhasÅ«tra III, 12.12, in this Å›rÄddha there is invitation to the brÄhmaá¹‡as the previous day, there is agnaukaraá¹‡a, there is the recital of Vedic texts when the brÄhmaá¹‡as are engaged in eating, VaiÅ›vadeva brÄhmaá¹‡as are honoured, the ViÅ›vedevas are KÄma and KÄla (vide n. 1018), there is the presentation of incense and lamp, there is svadhÄ and also namaskÄra. Four vessels for arghya should be got ready and filled with sandalwood paste, water and sesamum grains, one being for the deceased (the preta) and three for his paternal ancestors; two daiva brÄhmaá¹‡as should be invited, one to represent the preta and three to represent the three paternal ancestors of the preta; if a man is unable to invite so many brÄhmaá¹‡as, he should invite three, one for ViÅ›vedevas, one for the preta and one for the three paternal ancestors. He should request ‘I shall unite the vessel for the preta with the vessels for his three paternal ancestors.’ When permitted in the words ‘do unite’, he should cast the kuÅ›as in the vessel for the preta into the vessels for his three paternal ancestors, should keep a little water mixed with kuÅ›as in the vessel for the preta and distribute the rest of the water from the preta vessel into the three vessels for his ancestors with the two mantras20 ‘ye samÄnÄ’ (VÄj. Arghya is offered to the brÄhmaá¹‡a representing the preta with the water that remains in the preta vessel, arghya is offered to the three pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡as from the three pitá¹›ya vessels. Four piá¹‡á¸as should be prepared, one for the preta and three for his ancestors and then the performer requests “I shall unite the pretapiá¹‡á¸a with the piá¹‡á¸as for the three ancestors of his’; when permitted in the words ‘do unite’, the performer divides the pretapiá¹‡á¸a into three parts and puts each third into each of the three piá¹‡á¸as with the same two mantras (VÄj. The GarudapurÄá¹‡a (I. 220,6 ) strikes a discordant note in that it states that the pretapiá¹‡á¸a is to be divided in two parts, each of which is to be placed inside the piá¹‡á¸as for pitÄmaha and prapitÄmaha only.

##Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a

Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a partakes of the character of both ekoddiá¹£á¹­a and pÄrvaá¹‡a, the first applying to the preta and the 2nd to the three ancestors of the preta, so that in it two kinds of Å›rÄddhas are combined. When the sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a rite is completed by the giving of daká¹£iá¹‡Ä to the brÄhmaá¹‡as, then the preta ceases to be so and himself becomes a pitá¹›. The status of being a preta involves the experiencing of great torments due to hunger and thirst and becoming a pitá¹› means being brought in contact with the Å›rÄddha devatÄs called Vasu, Rudra and Ä€ditya21. The word preta has two meanings, (1) a person who is dead and (2) one who being dead has not the sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a performed for him. The result of sapiá¹‡á¸ana is that the22 great-grandfather of the deceased whose sapiá¹‡á¸ana is performed drops out from the list of pitá¹›s entitled to piá¹‡á¸a and becomes one called ’lepabhÄk’ (entitled to only wipings of the hand) and the former “preta becomes one of the pitá¹›s and entitled to participate in the piá¹‡á¸as offered at a pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha thereafter. As stated in the GarudapurÄá¹‡a I. 220. 2 the sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha is to be performed like the pÄrvaá¹‡a in the afternoon.

It may be noted that in some works the mantras recited at the time of pouring the water from the preta vessel into the pitá¹› vessels are different. For example, the Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh.S. 21. 14 says23 the mantras are ‘Samsá¹›jatu tvÄ pá¹›thivi’ (May the earth unite thee) and ‘samÄni va Äkutiá¸¥’ (á¹šg. (á¹šg. X. 191. 2-4, ‘May you unite’ &c.).

YÄj. (I. 254) and MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya (28. 17-18) provide that Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a and Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a Å›rÄddhas are performed for women also (but not pÄrvaá¹‡a nor Äbhyudayika). About the Sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a of the mother there are somewhat conflicting dicta. If a woman dies sonless and her husband is alive, her sapiá¹‡á¸ana is effected with her mother-in-law (GobhilasmrÌ¥ti II. 102). If a woman dies leaving no son and the husband also is dead, then no sapiá¹‡á¸ana can be performed for her. If she died either on the funeral pyre of her husband25 or afterwards (as a Sati) then her son should perform her sapiá¹‡á¸ana with her husband (i.e, the son’s father) and there is no separate sapiá¹‡á¸ana for her. If she was married in the Äsura form or she was made a putrikÄ, then the son should perform his mother’s sapiá¹‡á¸ana with his maternal grandfather; while a son born of a woman married in the BrÄhma and three other proper forms may perform her sapiá¹‡á¸ana with her husband or the paternal grandmother or the maternal grandfather. In the case of these three alternatives if there is a certain family usage that should be followed; otherwise there is an option. If a woman has a step-son, the latter should perform her sapiá¹‡á¸ana with his father, as Manu IX. 183 ( = Vas. 17. 11) indicates. Vide the Mit. on YÄj. I. 253-254 and Sm. C. (on ÄÅ›auca p. 169) for discussion of these points and for various alternatives26.

The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (III. p. 388) says that there is no sapiá¹‡á¸ana for one whose upanayana was not performed when he died, but if he was more than five years old then the 16 Å›rÄddhas are performed for him (though not sapiá¹‡á¸ana) and piá¹‡á¸a is offered on bare ground. It may be noted that no auspicious rite like marriage in which Äbhyudayika Å›rÄddha is necessary can be performed until the sapiá¹‡á¸ana of a deceased person of the family has been effected (except in the case of such absolutely necessary ceremonies as simantonnayana).

Manu (V. 89-90) prescribes that no water and other rites like sapiá¹‡á¸ikaraá¹‡a are offered and performed for those who had left off their faith, who were born of condemned mixed marriages, who entered ascetic orders, who had committed suicide (by hanging, poison &c.), who had embraced heretical doctrines, for women that wilfully seek illicit connection with men, that did harm to their foetus or husband (were guilty of abortion and murder of husband) and that were addicted to drinking surÄ. YÄj. III, 6 contains similar provisions. It has to be noted that every kind of suicide is not condemned by the ancient smrÌ¥tis. Vide H. of Dh. vol. III, pp. 939, 958â€“9 for cases where suicide was not condemned. Except in those cases Yama (q. by the Mit. on YÄj. Then the Mit. describes at length the procedure of NÄrÄyaá¹‡abali. Vide note 838 b p. 370 above for the SkandapurÄá¹‡a, NÄgarakhanda 219. 19-21 on the view that Å›rÄddha may be performed on the 14th of the dark half for persons who committed suicide or who met a violent death.

Ä€bhyudayika-Å›rÄddha

The Ä€bhyudayika-Å›rÄddha is to be now described. Ä€Å›v. gr. IV.7 mentions in one place only four Å›rÄddhas viz. PÄrvaá¹‡a, KÄmya, Ä€bhyudayika and Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a (note 966 above). Many of the sÅ«tras such as Ä€Å›v. gr. II. 5. 13-15, SÄn. gr. IV. 4, Gobhila gr. IV. 3. 35-37, Kauá¹£itaki gr. IV. 4, Baud. gr. According to most of these sutras this sraddha is performed when there is a lucky event such as the birth of a son, or his caula, upanayana or marriage or there 18the commencement of a charitable (pÅ«rta) act (such as the dedication of a well or a tank or a park to the public). Ä€Å›v.gr. and Gobhila gr. are very brief. They say that in this Å›rÄddha performed on auspicious occasions or on the under taking of meritorious acts an even number of brÄhmapas is to be fed, that the rite is to be performed from left to right and that yavas (barley ) are to be used instead of sesamum grains. As this Å›rÄddha is only a modification (viksti) of pÄrvaá¹‡a ( as said by AparÄrka p. 514) all rules of the latter will be applicable to the former except where special directions are given. The Ä€Å›v. gr. pariÅ›iá¹£á¹­a II. 19, Smá»™tyarthasÄra (p. 56), PitrdayitÄ pp. 62-71 give a compact but tolerably full description of this Å›rÄddha.

In this Å›rÄddha which is to be performed in the morning (except on the birth of a son when it is to be done at once ) the ViÅ›ve-devas are called Satya and Vasu, it is performed in the forenoon, the brÄhmaá¹‡as to be invited must be even in number, the darbhas are to be straight (and not doublefolded) and are not to be with their roots; the performer wears his sacred thread in the usual form ( and not in the prÄcinÄvita form), all actions are to be done from left to right (pradaká¹£iá¹‡am and not prasavyam); the word svadhÄ is not to be used; yava grains are to be employed instead of sesamum grains; he invites them with the words ‘find time to attend NÄndÄ«Å›rÄddha’. The brÄhmaá¹‡as say ‘be it so’. He says ‘May you two come ( to my house )’; they reply ‘we two shall come’. The performer faces the east or north (but never the south); the mantra about yavas is ‘yavosi’ (vide note 966 )27 he requests ‘I shall invoke the pitá¹›s called NÄndimukha’.28 When permitted by the brÄhmaá¹‡as in the words ‘do invoke’ he says ‘may the NÄndimukha pitá¹›s be pleased’; he offers arghya only once with the words ‘O NÄndÄ«mukha pitá¹›s! this is the arghya for you’. Sandalwood paste, incense, lamp are to be given twice; the homa is made on the hand of the brÄhmaá¹‡a. the two mantras being ’to Agni, the bearer of kavya! svÄhÄ’ and ’to Soma accompanied by the pitá¹›s! svÄhÄ.’

While the brÄhmaá¹‡as are eating the dinner, the RÄká¹£oghna mantras and mantras addressed to Indra and propitiatory mantras may be recited but not the mantras addressed to pitÅ™s (viz, á¹šg. The performer should ask ‘I shall request the nÄndimukha pitá¹›s to utter benedictions’ at the stage where (in PÄrvaá¹‡a) ‘aká¹£ayyodaka’ is asked for, to which the brÄhmaá¹‡as respond with the words ‘do request.’ The performer employs the word ‘sampannam’ (was it perfect) in asking about the gratification of the brÄhmaá¹‡as, who reply with the word ‘susampannam’ (it was quite perfect). On the brÄhmaá¹‡as sipping water after their dinner, he cowdungs the places where the dinner was taken, strews darbha grass thereon with their tips turned towards the east and offers thereon two piá¹‡á¸as for (each of the ancestors) made with the food that remains after the brÄhmaá¹‡as have eaten mixed with curds, jujube fruit and prÌ¥á¹£adÄjya29 (mixture of curds and clarified butter). The PratisÄmvatsarika or PratyÄbdika Å›rÄddha has already been described above at great length. It is to be performed every year on the day of death (GobhilasmrÌ¥ti III. 66 ). The MahÄlayaÅ›rÄddha

A well-known Å›rÄddha is the MahÄlayaÅ›rÄddha. Some of the PurÄá¹‡as deal with this. ÅšrÄddha by son of two fathers

Certain other matters connected with Å›rÄddhas may be briefly touched upon. In very ancient times twelve kinds of sons were recognized out of whom the ká¹£etraja60, the putrikÄputra and dattaka were most important. All these were the sons of two fathers. The question mooted was: to whom were they to offer piá¹‡á¸as? The MadanapÄrijÄta (pp. 607-608) quotes a passage from the HÄrÄ«ta-dharmasÅ«tra61 and explains it. HÄrÄ«ta says: ‘seed does not grow without a field. Since it is seen that both are necessary the child born is the child of both. Out of the two (fathers ) the procreator is to be first invoked (and then the ká¹£etrin). He (the son) may offer two piá¹‡das to each grade of the ancestors or he may offer a single piá¹‡á¸a (to the father and ) may repeat the names of both (fathers ) as to that single piá¹‡á¸a. The son of the son of two fathers ) may repeat two names as to the 2nd piá¹‡á¸a (i. e. the piá¹‡da for paternal grand-father ); the grand-son (of the son of two fathers ) may do the same as to the third piá¹‡á¸a (viz. the piá¹‡á¸a for paternal great-grandfather).

Manu IV. 140 and Gobhila-smrÌ¥ti II. 105 say about the putrikÄputra62 that he presents the first piá¹‡á¸a to his mother (as she was appointed as a son), the 2nd to her father and the third to his father’s father. This appears to be one order in which piá¹‡á¸as are to be offered by the putrikÄputra, while Manu IX. 132 sets out another method since it says that a putrikÄputra inherits the entire wealth of his own father if he is sonless and he gives two piá¹‡á¸as (i e. performs two Å›rÄddhas) for his own father and for his maternal grandfather. The SÄn. Åšr. S. provides63 that if there be two fathers, the son should recite the names of both (bijin and ká¹£etrin ) with reference to the same piá¹‡á¸a. Even YÄj. says “The son procreated according to the rules of niyoga by a sonless man on the wife of another inherits the wealth of both and offers piá¹‡da to both.’64 The Mit. adds that if a person appointed to procreate a son on another’s wife has a son, then the son so begotten becomes the son of the ká¹£etrin alone and not of the bijin. As the ká¹£etraja and putrikÄputra have become obsolete for several centuries the subject has only an academic interest now. But the dattaka is still in vogue and a few words must be said here about the persons to whom the dattaka offers piá¹‡á¸as.

The Kalpataru quotes from the PravarÄdhyÄya a passage on this point which is as follows:65 ‘If these (i.e. those who are bijin ) have no issue born of their own wives, (the sons begotten by niyoga or given in adoption ) should inherit their wealth and offer piá¹‡á¸as to them up to three ancestors; if both (the bijin and ká¹£etrin or the giver and taker in adoption) have no other son, then they (sons begotten or adopted ) should offer piá¹‡á¸a to both; in one and the same Å›rÄddha they should repeat (the names of) the two ancestors respectively (of the acceptor and that of the begetter) after having separately intended the same pipda for both up to the third ancestor.’ The Baud. Dh. Manu IX. 142 lays down ’the son given should not take the gotra and wealth of his natural father; the piá¹‡á¸a follows the gotra and wealth; the svadhÄ (obsequies) of him who gives (his son in adoption) cease or fail (so far as that son is concerned).’ This verse has been so interpreted by some decisions of the High Courts and of the Privy Council as to mean that the son adopted becomes totally severed from the family of his birth. This subject has been discussed at great length in H. of Dh. vol. The letting loose of a bull

Vá¹›á¹£otsarga (the letting loose of a bull). This topic has been dealt with by several sÅ«tra works such as the SÄn, gr. III. 11, the Kausitaki gr. III. 11 (in Benaras S. The description in the Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasutra being tolerably full is set out here;68

“(This ceremony) takes place on the full moon day in KÄrtika or Ä€Å›vina. In this rite he must first examine the bull. The bull must be the offspring of a milch cow having young ones living, he must have all auspicious marks (i.e. must not be deficient in any limb), must be dark coloured69 or red but having a white mouth, white tail, white feet and white horns, he must be one that can protect the herd (or that throws into the background by his height all the herd). Then after having kindled a blazing fire among the cows (in the cowpen) and having strewn kusa grass around it let him boil with milk a dish sacred to PÅ«á¹£an and offer (two oblations) with the mantra ‘May PÅ«á¹£an follow our cows’ (á¹šg. VI. 54.5) and the mantra ‘here is pleasure’ (VÄj. S. 8.51 ); a blacksmith should then mark the bull, on one flank with a discus and on the other flank70 with a trident. Let him wash the bull after he is marked with four mantras beginning with ’the golden-coloured’ (Tai. S. V. 6.1. 1-2) and with the (five) mantras beginning with ‘May the divine (waters) bring us happiness’ (á¹šg. X. 9. 4-8). Having washed and decked the bull he should bring him together with four young cows which also should have been washed and decked and mutter the Rudras (Tai. S. IV.5.1-11), the Puruá¹£asÅ«kta (á¹šg. 10.90.1-16 ) and the KÅ«á¹£mÄndis (Vaj. S. XX. 14-16, Tai. Ä€, X, 3-5). Then let him recite in the right ear of the bull the mantra71 ‘father of calves’ and the following mantras ‘The holy dharma72 is a bull and is declared to have four feet; I choose him with devotion (as the object of worship); may he protect me on all sides. This young bull I give you as husband (O young cows!), roam about sportingly with him as your lover. May we not lack progeny, O king Soma, nor physical fitness and may we not succumb to our enemy’.

He must drive away the bull together with the young cows in the north-eastern direction and give a pair of garments, gold and a vessel of bell metal to the hotá¹› (officiating priest). The blacksmith should be given wages as desired by him and food seasoned with a good deal of clarified butter should be served to (three) brÄhmaá¹‡as (at least). That73 pool at which a bull let loose (in honour of a deceased person by his son or the like) drinks water serves (reaches) the manes. Wherever a bull (let loose) exulting in his strength scratches (or digs up) the earth, that earth becoming abundant food and water waits upon the manes.”74 In the AnuÅ›Äsanaparva the pitá¹›s are represented as saying that by letting loose a dark coloured bull, by offering water mixed with sesame and by lighting lamps in the rains a man becomes free from the debt he owes to the pitá¹›s (chap. 125. 73-74).

In the GarudapurÄá¹‡a it is stated that the deceased person for whom a bull is not let loose on the 11th day after death permanently remains a preta, even if hundreds of Å›rÄddhas are offered for him. The same PurÄá¹‡a further provides that if a bull be not available on the 11th day, then the effigy of a bull made of darbhas and flour or clay should be symbolically let loose75. In the Bhaviá¹£yapurÄá¹‡a the 12th day after death is prescribed for the letting loose of a bull (N. S. III, p. 505 ). The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu says that the making of an effigy of a bull with clay, darbhas and flour is without authority. Even at present people let loose a bull but owing to the rise in the prices of bulls this is becoming less frequent.

ÅšrÄddha to himself by one living

Several medieval digests such as the Pitá¹›dayitÄ (pp. 84, 94), ÅšrÄddhaviveka of Rudradhara ( pp. 69-77), Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (III. pp. 595â€“596 ), SuddhiprakÄÅ›a he should himself bathe his body with (the water in) the jars with verses of Puruá¹£asÅ«kta and with paÃ±cagavya and pure water and eat in the evening food mixed with sesamum grains together with clarified butter. He should give dinner to brÄhmaá¹‡as for the gratification of the servants of Yama. On the 4th day he burns (the effigy) with mantras. He should offer water and piá¹‡á¸a with the words ‘piá¹‡á¸a to me of such and such a gotra for benefit in the next world; svadhÄ namaá¸¥.’ With these words he should bring to a close the rite. He should observe impurity for ten days for himself, but his agnates have not to observe impurity for him. On the 11th day he performs ekoddiá¹£á¹­a. They also cite the following verse ‘one who is in distress, a woman and a sÅ«dra having burnt one’s body (i.e effigy) with mantras should perform all rites on that very day. This is the revelation’. In the case of women the rite is performed silently or with (?) Vedic mantras. In this way he should perform his own Å›rÄddha every month for a year and at the end of each year up to twelve years. And then he should stop. When he is not able to do all these ( himself) his son and the like may perform them. They also recite the following verse; ‘Although heirs may be alive-one may perform one’s Å›rÄddha while alive, having quickly set about doing everything according to the rules, except Sapiá¹‡á¸ana. One should not make delay as to the time specified above, since life is evanescent’.

It may be noted that the Baud. gá¹›hyaÅ›eá¹£asÅ«tra III. 22 contains a very brief procedure of the same rite, but therein two verses of Kaá¹‡va and one of Viá¹£á¹‡u are quoted. It appears that this is a later addition. The text of Baud. gá¹›hyaÅ›eá¹£asÅ«tra III. 19 is quoted by Åšr. Pr. pp. 361-363. But the ÅšrÄddhaprakÄÅ›a also quotes a long passage from the Liá¹…gapurÄá¹‡a and explains it (pp. 363-368). The procedure in the Liá¹…gapurÄá¹‡a, however, materially differs from that of BaudhÄyana. It is passed over here from considerations of space. The ÅšraddhamayÅ«kha also gives an elaborate description. Some of its provisions are stated here. In Jivat-Å›rÄddha the word preta should nowhere be used. An effigy of the person is made with fifty kuÅ›as and is to be burnt by another with the mantra â€˜kravyÄdam-agnim’ (á¹šg. X. 16. 9). The person has to kindle fire on the banks of a river with his face to the south with his gá¹›hya fire or ordinary fire and has to dig up a pit and make prayer to the earth as in the case of an actual death.

In the Bombay University Library there is a Ms. of JivatÅ›rÄddha ascribed to Åšaunaka in the Bhadkamkar Collection.

It contains a far more elaborate procedure than that of BaudhÄyana and is in prose. It contains many of the provisions of BaudhÄyana. Further details are passed over here.

The provision of a Å›rÄddha for a living person offered by himself is a perversion of the ancient idea of Å›rÄddha. The basic and fundamental conception of a Å›rÄddha was to gratify the spirits of deceased ancestors. People had gone crazy with the idea of Å›rÄddhas and invented this new mode to satisfy that craze. I have known persons that performed Jivat-Å›rÄddha, though they had sons, younger brothers and nephews who would have certainly performed Å›rÄddhas for them on their death.

It is customary to make a gift of a cow with a calf to a brÄhmaá¹‡a, preferably a kapilÄ Ñow, on the 2nd day after the period of ÄÅ›auca. Often times this is the only cow given, and the cow called Vaitaraá¹‡i referred to above (p.183, note 427) is rarely donated in the midst of the sorrow and turmoil immediately on the death of a dear and near relative. A declaration is first made that a gift of a cow will be made and then water is poured on the hand of a brÄhmaá¹‡a. Then holding kuÅ›as in his hand the donor makes a gift of the cow with a formula noted below76. The donee replies with the words ‘om svasti’ (Yes, may it be well!). Then daká¹£inÄ (in gold or silver coins ) is given and the brÄhmaá¹‡a says ‘om svasti’, holds the tail of the cow and repeats a KÄmastuti (eulogy of KÄma)77 according to the recension of the Veda he has studied. The AnuÅ›Äsana parva ( 57. 28-29 ) eulogises the gift of a kapilÄ cow with the calf, given with a milking pail of bell-metal, whose horn tips are decked with gold, by stating that such a gift not only saves the donor in the other world but also his sons, grandsons and family for seven generations. The AnuÅ›Äsanaparva (77.10 ff) sets out a legend why the kapilÄ cow is the best of all cows.

The PurÄá¹‡as and digests devote a good deal of space to Å›rÄddhas at tirthas and at GayÄ. Vide Atri 55-58, VÄyupurÄá¹‡a 83. 16-42, HemÄdri (on Åšr.) pp. 1568 ff and 1575 ff. This subject will be briefly dealt with in the section on tirthas.

ÅšrÄddha in intercalary month

Much is said on the question whether Å›rÄddhas should be performed in the intercalary month. The intercalary month is called by various names, viz. Malimluca (KÄá¹­haka Saá¹. 38. 14),78 Saá¹sarpa or Aá¹hasaspati (Vaj.S:7.30 and 22.31), MalamÄsa, AdhimÄsa. An intercalary month is known even to the RÌ¥gveda (á¹šg. I. 25.8). The vendor of soma and the thirteenth month are condemned as pÄpa (sinful) in the Aitareya BrÄhmaá¹‡a79. The PurÄá¹‡as tried to bolster up the intercalary month by calling it Puruá¹£ottama-mÄsa (i.e. the month of Viá¹£á¹‡u) but the original stigma attaching to the 13th month seems to have persisted. The general rule about an intercalary month is stated by the GrÌ¥hyapariÅ›iá¹£á¹­a:80 ’the month called Malimluca is polluted and springs from sin; it is condemned for all acts; it should be discarded in all rites in honour of gods and pitá¹›s’. But exceptions to this sweeping prohibition have been recognized. HÄrÄ«ta provides81 that all Å›rÄddhas that come after Sapiá¹‡á¸ana are not to be performed in an intercalary month, VyÄsa laid down82 that such ceremonies as jÄtakarma, annaprÄÅ›ana, the navaÅ›rÄddhas, Å›raddha on MaghÄ and the thirteenth tithi, the sixteen Å›rÄddhas, bath, gifts, japa and Å›rÄddha at the time of the eclipse of the sun or moon-these should be performed even in an intercalary month. The SmrÌ¥timuktÄphala concludes (p. 728 ) that there is no blemish in performing a Å›rÄddha in an intercalary month provided it is to be performed before the end of one year from death.

It is provided by BhrÌ¥gu83 that the sÄá¹vatsarika Å›rÄddha of those who die in an intercalary month may be performed in the intercalary month, but if there be none such then in the ordinary month of the same name. When the tithi for performing a Å›rÄddha comes and there is then an intercalary month, VrÌ¥ddha-Vasistha says that Å›rÄddha should be performed in both months84.

Further discussion about what may be done in MalamÄsa and what is prohibited therein will be taken up in the section on KÄla. The question as to the day on which a Å›rÄddha is to be performed if the tithi is spread over two days or if there is a ká¹£aya of the tithi will also be discussed in that section.

Some of the works on Å›rÄddha such as the PrÌ¥thvi-candrodaya deal with what is called ‘saá¹…ghÄta-Å›rÄddha’85. When on the same day several persons die, but not at the same time, then the Å›rÄddhas for them should be performed, as said by Rá¹£yaÅ›rÌ¥á¹…ga, in the order in which they died. But if five or six persons die at the same time on the same day (as when a vessel sinks in the sea or a market is burnt down all at once ), then the order in which Å›rÄddhas are to be performed is determined by the nearness of relationship to the performer of Å›rÄddha in the case of each. For example, if a person’s wife, son, brother and paternal uncle meet death at the same time, then the order should be this that the Å›rÄddha of the wife is performed first. then of the son, then of the brother and then of the uncle. If the parents die at the same time by accident, then the father’s cremation or other rites should be first performed and then of the mother.

Provision is made if the performance of a Å›rÄddha is rendered impossible by some obstacle. Rá¹£yaÅ›rÌ¥á¹…ga provides86 ‘if impurity (on death ) intervenes when a Å›rÄddha is to be offered to the pitá¹›s, Å›rÄddha should be offered at the end of the period of impurity. If an obstacle arises at the time when an ekoddiá¹£á¹­a is to be performed, the Å›rÄddha should be performed in another month on the same tithi’. This last refers to a monthly Å›rÄddha.

If any of the sixteen Å›rÄddhas fails owing to an obstacle it should be performed on the amÄvÄsyÄ or better still on the 11th day of the dark half. If there is an obstacle owing to impurity on death in the performance of a monthly Å›rÄddha or yearly Å›rÄddha it should be performed at the end of the impurity or on amÄvÄsyÄ87. The PadmapurÄá¹‡a (PÄtÄlakhaá¹‡á¸a 101. 68-70) provides similar rules. If the obstacle is the performer’s illness or his inability to collect materials or his wife being in her monthly illness, he may offer ÄmaÅ›rÄddha described above.

It may be noted that while great emphasis was laid on inviting a very learned brÄhmaá¹‡a at a Å›rÄddha, that object was often frustrated in practice by the provisions made in certain smrÌ¥tis that one88 should not partake of Å›rÄddha food for three years after sapiá¹‡á¸ana (which is often one year after death) and that by dining at a Å›rÄddha in the first year one eats the bones and marrow of the deceased, in the second year his flesh. in the third year his blood and Å›rÄddha in the 4th year is (somewhat ) pure. Vide Par. M. vol. II part 1 p. 423, where smrÌ¥ti passages prescribing prÄyaÅ›cittas for partaking of food at various Å›rÄddhas including sÄá¹vatsarika are set out. HÄrita says: one partaking of food at NavaÅ›rÄddha should undergo CÄndrÄyaá¹‡a, the PrÄjÄpatya for dining at a monthly Å›rÄddha and fast for one day for dining at a pratyÄbdika Å›rÄddha. This is on the same lines as acceptance of gifts. A donor collected merit by making gifts, but it was for the acceptor to decide whether he should accept gifts.

High ideal for brÄhmaá¹‡as

The ideal placed before brÄhmaá¹‡as was that one, though entitled to accept gifts on account of his vedic learning and tapas, should not accept gifts, if he desires to secure the highest world (YÄj. I. 213), while Manu IV. 186 affirms89 that though entitled to accept gifts a brÄhmaá¹‡a should not again and again resort to that method, since the spiritual power that he acquires by Vedic study is lost by accepting gifts. Manu IV. 85-86 (= Padma V. 19. 236-237) say that acceptance of a gift from the king is terrible (in its consequences) and Padma warns that gift appears sweet like honey but is like poison (i.e. deadly in its effects). This reasoning applied with greater force to officiating and dining at a Å›rÄddha, where not only gifts are to be received, but also sumptuous food that will please the palate is served in abundance.

It has been seen above how the most ancient literary monument, viz. the á¹šgveda, shows that the funeral rites performed immediately after death were prompted by affectionate concern for the departed spirit mingled with some element of fear, that the object of those rites was to provide sustenance to the departed spirit and to endow it with an intermediate body before it became one of the pitá¹›s. It has also been found that in the most ancient times of which we have literary evidence, there were rites for the worship of ancestors, being the Piá¹‡á¸apitá¹›yajÃ±a performed every month on amÄvÄsyÄ, the MahÄpitá¹› yajÃ±a performed in the SÄkamedha and the Aá¹£á¹­akÄ Å›rÄddhas. Gradually the rites for deceased ancestors became more and more frequent, all-inclusive and elaborate, so that ultimately an exaggerated importance came to be attached to the cult of Å›rÄddhas over which there was an enormous expenditure of time, effort and wealth. The question now arises what Indians of the 20th century should do about Å›rÄddhas. One finds that in these days when many brÄhmaá¹‡as even do not engage in any of the five daily obligatory yajÃ±as (the paÃ±ca-yajÃ±as), they are solicitous to offer Å›rÄddha at least once a year to their ancestors. The following may be therefore suggested as a via media for all kinds of people.

Those who have faith in the duty of performing Å›rÄddhas and their efficacy for the benefit of the dead may perform them on a small scale, bearing in mind the emphatic admonition of Manu (III. 125-126 ), the KÅ«rmapurÄá¹‡a II. 22. 27, PadmapurÄá¹‡a V. 9. 9890 that one should desist from extravagance in the matter of Å›rÄddhas, particularly in the number of brÄhmaá¹‡as to be invited. Those whose faith is shaken or shattered by the onrush of modern ideas and English education or firm belief in the doctrines of karma and punarjanma should also remember one thing. The main underlying conception of Å›rÄddha is certainly admirable, viz, a tender and affectionate regard for one’s near and dear relatives. It is a good practice to set apart at least one day in a year for the remembrance of one’s near and dear relatives that are no more, to invite relatives, friends, and learned people to a dinner in memory of the dead and to bestow monetary gifts on poor but learned persons of character and devoted to the practice of plain living and high thinking. This will be in keeping with our past traditions and will also give a new orientation to and infuse new life into practices and usages that have become lifeless and meaningless to many people. From very ancient times one of the fundamental conceptions of our faith has been the idea of three debts owed to sages, gods and pitá¹›s. The debt owed to the ancestors is paid off by the procreation of a son who would offer piá¹‡á¸as to his (and therefore also to his father’s) ancestors. This is a grand conception. Nothing can be more sublime than the formula which one has to repeat at the time of offering piá¹‡á¸as and water (with sesame) at GayÄ ‘may those of my ancestors that are in the form of pretas be all satiated by means of the (balls made) of barley flour mixed with sesame and may everything, whether moving or immoveable from BrahmÄ up to blades of grass derive satisfaction from the water offered by me.’

If the implication of this formula be thoroughly understood and implemented by actual practice, it will make the whole world kin. Therefore, while discarding the heavy accretions accumulated through ages, we Hindus of these days must see to it that we do not throw overboard the gold that lies buried under the crust of ritual and ill-understood ceremonies.*

*In passing I may mention that my friend Mr. N. G. Chapekar B. A., LL.B, retired First Class Subordinate Judge residing at Badlapur in the Thana District, has been celebrating the yearly Å›rÄddha of his mother in the manner indicated above for about twenty-five years.

  39. The worship of the mother Goddess or of mother Goddesses is one of the oldest and most widespread forms of religion. The mother Goddess appears in the civilizations of Mesopotamia and Syria, in prehistoric Europe and west Africa. Dikshitit Poona). ↩︎

  60. The ká¹£etraja son was procreated on the wife or widow of a sonless man by a sagotra (a brother or other agnate) or even an asagotra according to the rules of niyoga, the procreator being called bijin and the husband on whose wife or widow the son was begotten was called ká¹£etrin. The putrikÄputra is of two kinds, (1) a sonless man gives his daughter in marriage to another with the stipulation that the son born of the marriage will be the son of the girl’s father (Vas. 17. 17, Manu IX. 127); (2) A daughter herself may be made a son (Vas. 17. 16). A dattaka is a son whom his father or mother gives to another as a son confirming the gift with water (Manu IX, 168). Vide H. of Dh. vol. III. pp. 647-650 for detailed explanations about these and other secondary sons. ↩︎
